2 The young Queen was turned of twenty , not reckoning the hundred years she had been asleep ; and how to find in the yard a beast so firm was what puzzled him .
3 He took then a resolution , that he might save his own life , to cut the Queen 's throat ; and going up into her chamber , with intent to do it at once , he put himself into as great fury as he could possibly , and came into the young Queen 's room with his dagger in his hand .
4 He would not , however , surprise her , but told her , with a great deal of respect , the orders he had received from the Queen-mother .
5 `` Do it ; do it '' -LRB- said she , stretching out her neck -RRB- .
6 `` Execute your orders , and then I shall go and see my children , my poor children , whom I so much and so tenderly loved . ''
7 For she thought them dead ever since they had been taken away without her knowledge .
8 `` No , no , madam '' -LRB- cried the poor clerk of the kitchen , all in tears -RRB- ; `` you shall not die , and yet you shall see your children again ; but then you must go home with me to my lodgings , where I have concealed them , and I shall deceive the Queen once more , by giving her in your stead a young hind . ''
9 Upon this he forthwith conducted her to his chamber , where , leaving her to embrace her children , and cry along with them , he went and dressed a young hind , which the Queen had for her supper , and devoured it with the same appetite as if it had been the young Queen .
10 Exceedingly was she delighted with her cruelty , and she had invented a story to tell the King , at his return , how the mad wolves had eaten up the Queen his wife and her two children .
11 One evening , as she was , according to her custom , rambling round about the courts and yards of the palace to see if she could smell any fresh meat , she heard , in a ground room , little Day crying , for his mamma was going to whip him , because he had been naughty ; and she heard , at the same time , little Morning begging pardon for her brother .
12 The Ogress presently knew the voice of the Queen and her children , and being quite mad that she had been thus deceived , she commanded next morning , by break of day -LRB- with a most horrible voice , which made everybody tremble -RRB- , that they should bring into the middle of the great court a large tub , which she caused to be filled with toads , vipers , snakes , and all sorts of serpents , in order to have thrown into it the Queen and her children , the clerk of the kitchen , his wife and maid ; all whom she had given orders should be brought thither with their hands tied behind them .
13 They were brought out accordingly , and the executioners were just going to throw them into the tub , when the King -LRB- who was not so soon expected -RRB- entered the court on horseback -LRB- for he came post -RRB- and asked , with the utmost astonishment , what was the meaning of that horrible spectacle .
14 No one dared to tell him , when the Ogress , all enraged to see what had happened , threw herself head foremost into the tub , and was instantly devoured by the ugly creatures she had ordered to be thrown into it for others .
15 The King could not but be very sorry , for she was his mother ; but he soon comforted himself with his beautiful wife and his pretty children .
16 CINDERELLA , OR THE LITTLE GLASS SLIPPER Once there was a gentleman who married , for his second wife , the proudest and most haughty woman that was ever seen .
17 She had , by a former husband , two daughters of her own humor , who were , indeed , exactly like her in all things .
18 He had likewise , by another wife , a young daughter , but of unparalleled goodness and sweetness of temper , which she took from her mother , who was the best creature in the world .
19 No sooner were the ceremonies of the wedding over but the mother-in-law began to show herself in her true colors .
20 She could not bear the good qualities of this pretty girl , and the less because they made her own daughters appear the more odious .
21 She employed her in the meanest work of the house : she scoured the dishes , tables , etc. , and scrubbed madam 's chamber , and those of misses , her XXXXX ; she lay up in a sorry garret , upon a wretched straw bed , while her sisters lay in fine rooms , with floors all inlaid , upon beds of the very newest fashion , and where they had looking-glasses so large that they might see themselves at their full length from head to foot .	daughters		beast|clerk|daughters|everybody|hind|kitchen|others|palace|snakes|voice

1 The young Queen was turned of twenty , not reckoning the hundred years she had been asleep ; and how to find in the yard a beast so firm was what puzzled him .
2 He took then a resolution , that he might save his own life , to cut the Queen 's throat ; and going up into her chamber , with intent to do it at once , he put himself into as great fury as he could possibly , and came into the young Queen 's room with his dagger in his hand .
3 He would not , however , surprise her , but told her , with a great deal of respect , the orders he had received from the Queen-mother .
4 `` Do it ; do it '' -LRB- said she , stretching out her neck -RRB- .
5 `` Execute your orders , and then I shall go and see my children , my poor children , whom I so much and so tenderly loved . ''
6 For she thought them dead ever since they had been taken away without her knowledge .
7 `` No , no , madam '' -LRB- cried the poor clerk of the kitchen , all in tears -RRB- ; `` you shall not die , and yet you shall see your children again ; but then you must go home with me to my lodgings , where I have concealed them , and I shall deceive the Queen once more , by giving her in your stead a young hind . ''
8 Upon this he forthwith conducted her to his chamber , where , leaving her to embrace her children , and cry along with them , he went and dressed a young hind , which the Queen had for her supper , and devoured it with the same appetite as if it had been the young Queen .
9 Exceedingly was she delighted with her cruelty , and she had invented a story to tell the King , at his return , how the mad wolves had eaten up the Queen his wife and her two children .
10 One evening , as she was , according to her custom , rambling round about the courts and yards of the palace to see if she could smell any fresh meat , she heard , in a ground room , little Day crying , for his mamma was going to whip him , because he had been naughty ; and she heard , at the same time , little Morning begging pardon for her brother .
11 The Ogress presently knew the voice of the Queen and her children , and being quite mad that she had been thus deceived , she commanded next morning , by break of day -LRB- with a most horrible voice , which made everybody tremble -RRB- , that they should bring into the middle of the great court a large tub , which she caused to be filled with toads , vipers , snakes , and all sorts of serpents , in order to have thrown into it the Queen and her children , the clerk of the kitchen , his wife and maid ; all whom she had given orders should be brought thither with their hands tied behind them .
12 They were brought out accordingly , and the executioners were just going to throw them into the tub , when the King -LRB- who was not so soon expected -RRB- entered the court on horseback -LRB- for he came post -RRB- and asked , with the utmost astonishment , what was the meaning of that horrible spectacle .
13 No one dared to tell him , when the Ogress , all enraged to see what had happened , threw herself head foremost into the tub , and was instantly devoured by the ugly creatures she had ordered to be thrown into it for others .
14 The King could not but be very sorry , for she was his mother ; but he soon comforted himself with his beautiful wife and his pretty children .
15 CINDERELLA , OR THE LITTLE GLASS SLIPPER Once there was a gentleman who married , for his second wife , the proudest and most haughty woman that was ever seen .
16 She had , by a former husband , two daughters of her own humor , who were , indeed , exactly like her in all things .
17 He had likewise , by another wife , a young daughter , but of unparalleled goodness and sweetness of temper , which she took from her mother , who was the best creature in the world .
18 No sooner were the ceremonies of the wedding over but the mother-in-law began to show herself in her true colors .
19 She could not bear the good qualities of this pretty girl , and the less because they made her own daughters appear the more odious .
20 She employed her in the meanest work of the house : she scoured the dishes , tables , etc. , and scrubbed madam 's chamber , and those of misses , her daughters ; she lay up in a sorry garret , upon a wretched straw bed , while her sisters lay in fine rooms , with floors all inlaid , upon beds of the very newest fashion , and where they had looking-glasses so large that they might see themselves at their full length from head to foot .
21 The poor XXXXX bore all patiently , and dared not tell her father , who would have rattled her off ; for his wife governed him entirely .	girl		Ogress|custom|girl|hand|house|life|meat|one|palace|things

4 CINDERELLA , OR THE LITTLE GLASS SLIPPER Once there was a gentleman who married , for his second wife , the proudest and most haughty woman that was ever seen .
5 She had , by a former husband , two daughters of her own humor , who were , indeed , exactly like her in all things .
6 He had likewise , by another wife , a young daughter , but of unparalleled goodness and sweetness of temper , which she took from her mother , who was the best creature in the world .
7 No sooner were the ceremonies of the wedding over but the mother-in-law began to show herself in her true colors .
8 She could not bear the good qualities of this pretty girl , and the less because they made her own daughters appear the more odious .
9 She employed her in the meanest work of the house : she scoured the dishes , tables , etc. , and scrubbed madam 's chamber , and those of misses , her daughters ; she lay up in a sorry garret , upon a wretched straw bed , while her sisters lay in fine rooms , with floors all inlaid , upon beds of the very newest fashion , and where they had looking-glasses so large that they might see themselves at their full length from head to foot .
10 The poor girl bore all patiently , and dared not tell her father , who would have rattled her off ; for his wife governed him entirely .
11 When she had done her work , she used to go into the chimney-corner , and sit down among cinders and ashes , which made her commonly be called Cinderwench ; but the youngest , who was not so rude and uncivil as the eldest , called her Cinderella .
12 However , Cinderella , notwithstanding her mean apparel , was a hundred times handsomer than her sisters , though they were always dressed very richly .
13 It happened that the King 's son gave a ball , and invited all persons of fashion to it .
14 Our young misses were also invited , for they cut a very grand figure among the quality .
15 They were mightily delighted at this invitation , and wonderfully busy in choosing out such gowns , petticoats , and head-clothes as might become them .
16 This was a new trouble to Cinderella ; for it was she who ironed her sisters ' linen , and plaited their ruffles ; they talked all day long of nothing but how they should be dressed .
17 `` For my part , '' said the eldest , `` I will wear my red velvet suit with French trimming . ''
18 `` And I , '' said the youngest , `` shall have my usual petticoat ; but then , to make amends for that , I will put on my gold-flowered manteau , and my diamond stomacher , which is far from being the most ordinary one in the world . ''
19 They sent for the best tire-woman they could get to make up their head-dresses and adjust their double pinners , and they had their red brushes and patches from Mademoiselle de la Poche .
20 Cinderella was likewise called up to them to be consulted in all these matters , for she had excellent notions , and advised them always for the best , nay , and offered her services to dress their heads , which they were very willing she should do .
21 As she was doing this , they said to her : `` Cinderella , would you not be glad to go to the XXXXX ? ''	ball		ball|chamber|daughters|humor|invitation|mother-in-law|others|qualities|wedding|work

1 She had , by a former husband , two daughters of her own humor , who were , indeed , exactly like her in all things .
2 He had likewise , by another wife , a young daughter , but of unparalleled goodness and sweetness of temper , which she took from her mother , who was the best creature in the world .
3 No sooner were the ceremonies of the wedding over but the mother-in-law began to show herself in her true colors .
4 She could not bear the good qualities of this pretty girl , and the less because they made her own daughters appear the more odious .
5 She employed her in the meanest work of the house : she scoured the dishes , tables , etc. , and scrubbed madam 's chamber , and those of misses , her daughters ; she lay up in a sorry garret , upon a wretched straw bed , while her sisters lay in fine rooms , with floors all inlaid , upon beds of the very newest fashion , and where they had looking-glasses so large that they might see themselves at their full length from head to foot .
6 The poor girl bore all patiently , and dared not tell her father , who would have rattled her off ; for his wife governed him entirely .
7 When she had done her work , she used to go into the chimney-corner , and sit down among cinders and ashes , which made her commonly be called Cinderwench ; but the youngest , who was not so rude and uncivil as the eldest , called her Cinderella .
8 However , Cinderella , notwithstanding her mean apparel , was a hundred times handsomer than her sisters , though they were always dressed very richly .
9 It happened that the King 's son gave a ball , and invited all persons of fashion to it .
10 Our young misses were also invited , for they cut a very grand figure among the quality .
11 They were mightily delighted at this invitation , and wonderfully busy in choosing out such gowns , petticoats , and head-clothes as might become them .
12 This was a new trouble to Cinderella ; for it was she who ironed her sisters ' linen , and plaited their ruffles ; they talked all day long of nothing but how they should be dressed .
13 `` For my part , '' said the eldest , `` I will wear my red velvet suit with French trimming . ''
14 `` And I , '' said the youngest , `` shall have my usual petticoat ; but then , to make amends for that , I will put on my gold-flowered manteau , and my diamond stomacher , which is far from being the most ordinary one in the world . ''
15 They sent for the best tire-woman they could get to make up their head-dresses and adjust their double pinners , and they had their red brushes and patches from Mademoiselle de la Poche .
16 Cinderella was likewise called up to them to be consulted in all these matters , for she had excellent notions , and advised them always for the best , nay , and offered her services to dress their heads , which they were very willing she should do .
17 As she was doing this , they said to her : `` Cinderella , would you not be glad to go to the ball ? ''
18 `` Alas ! ''
19 said she , `` you only jeer me ; it is not for such as I am to go thither . ''
20 `` Thou art in the right of it , '' replied they ; `` it would make the people laugh to see a Cinderwench at a ball . ''
21 Anyone but Cinderella would have dressed their XXXXX awry , but she was very good , and dressed them perfectly well They were almost two days without eating , so much were they transported with joy .	heads		ashes|cinders|colors|daughters|diamond|figure|goodness|heads|invitation|qualities

1 She employed her in the meanest work of the house : she scoured the dishes , tables , etc. , and scrubbed madam 's chamber , and those of misses , her daughters ; she lay up in a sorry garret , upon a wretched straw bed , while her sisters lay in fine rooms , with floors all inlaid , upon beds of the very newest fashion , and where they had looking-glasses so large that they might see themselves at their full length from head to foot .
2 The poor girl bore all patiently , and dared not tell her father , who would have rattled her off ; for his wife governed him entirely .
3 When she had done her work , she used to go into the chimney-corner , and sit down among cinders and ashes , which made her commonly be called Cinderwench ; but the youngest , who was not so rude and uncivil as the eldest , called her Cinderella .
4 However , Cinderella , notwithstanding her mean apparel , was a hundred times handsomer than her sisters , though they were always dressed very richly .
5 It happened that the King 's son gave a ball , and invited all persons of fashion to it .
6 Our young misses were also invited , for they cut a very grand figure among the quality .
7 They were mightily delighted at this invitation , and wonderfully busy in choosing out such gowns , petticoats , and head-clothes as might become them .
8 This was a new trouble to Cinderella ; for it was she who ironed her sisters ' linen , and plaited their ruffles ; they talked all day long of nothing but how they should be dressed .
9 `` For my part , '' said the eldest , `` I will wear my red velvet suit with French trimming . ''
10 `` And I , '' said the youngest , `` shall have my usual petticoat ; but then , to make amends for that , I will put on my gold-flowered manteau , and my diamond stomacher , which is far from being the most ordinary one in the world . ''
11 They sent for the best tire-woman they could get to make up their head-dresses and adjust their double pinners , and they had their red brushes and patches from Mademoiselle de la Poche .
12 Cinderella was likewise called up to them to be consulted in all these matters , for she had excellent notions , and advised them always for the best , nay , and offered her services to dress their heads , which they were very willing she should do .
13 As she was doing this , they said to her : `` Cinderella , would you not be glad to go to the ball ? ''
14 `` Alas ! ''
15 said she , `` you only jeer me ; it is not for such as I am to go thither . ''
16 `` Thou art in the right of it , '' replied they ; `` it would make the people laugh to see a Cinderwench at a ball . ''

1 The poor girl bore all patiently , and dared not tell her father , who would have rattled her off ; for his wife governed him entirely .
2 When she had done her work , she used to go into the chimney-corner , and sit down among cinders and ashes , which made her commonly be called Cinderwench ; but the youngest , who was not so rude and uncivil as the eldest , called her Cinderella .
3 However , Cinderella , notwithstanding her mean apparel , was a hundred times handsomer than her sisters , though they were always dressed very richly .
4 It happened that the King 's son gave a ball , and invited all persons of fashion to it .
5 Our young misses were also invited , for they cut a very grand figure among the quality .
6 They were mightily delighted at this invitation , and wonderfully busy in choosing out such gowns , petticoats , and head-clothes as might become them .
7 This was a new trouble to Cinderella ; for it was she who ironed her sisters ' linen , and plaited their ruffles ; they talked all day long of nothing but how they should be dressed .
8 `` For my part , '' said the eldest , `` I will wear my red velvet suit with French trimming . ''
9 `` And I , '' said the youngest , `` shall have my usual petticoat ; but then , to make amends for that , I will put on my gold-flowered manteau , and my diamond stomacher , which is far from being the most ordinary one in the world . ''
10 They sent for the best tire-woman they could get to make up their head-dresses and adjust their double pinners , and they had their red brushes and patches from Mademoiselle de la Poche .
11 Cinderella was likewise called up to them to be consulted in all these matters , for she had excellent notions , and advised them always for the best , nay , and offered her services to dress their heads , which they were very willing she should do .
12 As she was doing this , they said to her : `` Cinderella , would you not be glad to go to the ball ? ''
13 `` Alas ! ''
14 said she , `` you only jeer me ; it is not for such as I am to go thither . ''
15 `` Thou art in the right of it , '' replied they ; `` it would make the people laugh to see a Cinderwench at a ball . ''
16 Anyone but Cinderella would have dressed their heads awry , but she was very good , and dressed them perfectly well They were almost two days without eating , so much were they transported with joy .
17 They broke above a dozen laces in trying to be laced up close , that they might have a fine slender shape , and they were continually at their looking-glass .
18 At last the happy day came ; they went to Court , and Cinderella followed them with her eyes as long as she could , and when she had lost sight of them , she fell a-crying .
19 Her godmother , who saw her all in tears , asked her what was the matter .
20 `` I wish I could -- I wish I could -- '' ; she was not able to speak the rest , being interrupted by her tears and sobbing .

3 `` Thou art in the right of it , '' replied they ; `` it would make the people laugh to see a Cinderwench at a ball . ''
4 Anyone but Cinderella would have dressed their heads awry , but she was very good , and dressed them perfectly well They were almost two days without eating , so much were they transported with joy .
5 They broke above a dozen laces in trying to be laced up close , that they might have a fine slender shape , and they were continually at their looking-glass .
6 At last the happy day came ; they went to Court , and Cinderella followed them with her eyes as long as she could , and when she had lost sight of them , she fell a-crying .
7 Her godmother , who saw her all in tears , asked her what was the matter .
8 `` I wish I could -- I wish I could -- '' ; she was not able to speak the rest , being interrupted by her tears and sobbing .
9 This godmother of hers , who was a fairy , said to her , `` Thou wishest thou couldst go to the ball ; is it not so ? ''
10 `` Y -- es , '' cried Cinderella , with a great sigh .
11 `` Well , '' said her godmother , `` be but a good girl , and I will contrive that thou shalt go . ''
12 Then she took her into her chamber , and said to her , `` Run into the garden , and bring me a pumpkin . ''
13 Cinderella went immediately to gather the finest she could get , and brought it to her godmother , not being able to imagine how this pumpkin could make her go to the ball .
14 Her godmother scooped out all the inside of it , having left nothing but the rind ; which done , she struck it with her wand , and the pumpkin was instantly turned into a fine coach , gilded all over with gold .
15 She then went to look into her mouse-trap , where she found six mice , all alive , and ordered Cinderella to lift up a little the trapdoor , when , giving each mouse , as it went out , a little tap with her wand , the mouse was that moment turned into a fine horse , which altogether made a very fine set of six horses of a beautiful mouse-colored dapple-gray .
16 Being at a loss for a coachman , `` I will go and see , '' says Cinderella , `` if there is never a rat in the rat-trap -- we may make a coachman of him . ''
17 `` Thou art in the right , '' replied her godmother ; `` go and look . ''

11 The fairy made choice of one of the three which had the largest beard , and , having touched him with her wand , he was turned into a fat , jolly coachman , who had the smartest whiskers eyes ever beheld .
12 After that , she said to her : `` Go again into the garden , and you will find six lizards behind the watering-pot , bring them to me . ''
13 She had no sooner done so but her godmother turned them into six footmen , who skipped up immediately behind the coach , with their liveries all bedaubed with gold and silver , and clung as close behind each other as if they had done nothing else their whole lives .
14 The Fairy then said to Cinderella : `` Well , you see here an equipage fit to go to the ball with ; are you not pleased with it ? ''
15 `` Oh !
16 yes , '' cried she ; `` but must I go thither as I am , in these nasty rags ? ''
17 Her godmother only just touched her with her wand , and , at the same instant , her clothes were turned into cloth of gold and silver , all beset with jewels .
18 This done , she gave her a pair of glass slippers , the prettiest in the whole world .
19 Being thus decked out , she got up into her coach ; but her godmother , above all things , commanded her not to stay till after midnight , telling her , at the same time , that if she stayed one moment longer , the coach would be a pumpkin again , her horses mice , her coachman a rat , her footmen lizards , and her clothes become just as they were before .
20 She promised her godmother she would not fail of leaving the ball before midnight ; and then away she drives , scarce able to contain herself for joy .
21 The King 's son who was told that a great princess , whom nobody knew , was come , ran out to receive her ; he gave her his hand as she alighted out of the coach , and led her into the XXXXX , among all the company .	ball		Run|Y|ball|clothes|coachman|lives|mice|pumpkin|set|time

1 She then went to look into her mouse-trap , where she found six mice , all alive , and ordered Cinderella to lift up a little the trapdoor , when , giving each mouse , as it went out , a little tap with her wand , the mouse was that moment turned into a fine horse , which altogether made a very fine set of six horses of a beautiful mouse-colored dapple-gray .
2 Being at a loss for a coachman , `` I will go and see , '' says Cinderella , `` if there is never a rat in the rat-trap -- we may make a coachman of him . ''
3 `` Thou art in the right , '' replied her godmother ; `` go and look . ''

14 A fine collation was served up , whereof the young prince ate not a morsel , so intently was he busied in gazing on her .
15 She went and sat down by her sisters , showing them a thousand civilities , giving them part of the oranges and citrons which the Prince had presented her with , which very much surprised them , for they did not know her .
16 While Cinderella was thus amusing her sisters , she heard the clock strike eleven and three-quarters , whereupon she immediately made a courtesy to the company and hasted away as fast as she could .
17 When she got home she ran to seek out her godmother , and , after having thanked her , she said she could not but heartily wish she might go next day to the ball , because the King 's son had desired her .
18 As she was eagerly telling her godmother whatever had passed at the ball , her two sisters knocked at the door , which Cinderella ran and opened .
19 `` How long you have stayed ! ''
20 cried she , gaping , rubbing her eyes and stretching herself as if she had been just waked out of her sleep ; she had not , however , any manner of inclination to sleep since they went from home .

1 Her godmother only just touched her with her wand , and , at the same instant , her clothes were turned into cloth of gold and silver , all beset with jewels .
2 This done , she gave her a pair of glass slippers , the prettiest in the whole world .
3 Being thus decked out , she got up into her coach ; but her godmother , above all things , commanded her not to stay till after midnight , telling her , at the same time , that if she stayed one moment longer , the coach would be a pumpkin again , her horses mice , her coachman a rat , her footmen lizards , and her clothes become just as they were before .
4 She promised her godmother she would not fail of leaving the ball before midnight ; and then away she drives , scarce able to contain herself for joy .
5 The King 's son who was told that a great princess , whom nobody knew , was come , ran out to receive her ; he gave her his hand as she alighted out of the coach , and led her into the ball , among all the company .
6 There was immediately a profound silence , they left off dancing , and the violins ceased to play , so attentive was everyone to contemplate the singular beauties of the unknown new-comer .
7 Nothing was then heard but a confused noise of : `` Ha !
8 how handsome she is !
9 Ha !
10 how handsome she is ! ''
11 The King himself , old as he was , could not help watching her , and telling the Queen softly that it was a long time since he had seen so beautiful and lovely a creature .
12 All the ladies were busied in considering her clothes and headdress , that they might have some made next day after the same pattern , provided they could meet with such fine material and as able hands to make them .
13 The King 's son conducted her to the most honorable seat , and afterward took her out to dance with him ; she danced so very gracefully that they all more and more admired her .
14 A fine collation was served up , whereof the young prince ate not a morsel , so intently was he busied in gazing on her .
15 She went and sat down by her sisters , showing them a thousand civilities , giving them part of the oranges and citrons which the Prince had presented her with , which very much surprised them , for they did not know her .
16 While Cinderella was thus amusing her sisters , she heard the clock strike eleven and three-quarters , whereupon she immediately made a courtesy to the company and hasted away as fast as she could .
17 When she got home she ran to seek out her godmother , and , after having thanked her , she said she could not but heartily wish she might go next day to the ball , because the King 's son had desired her .
18 As she was eagerly telling her godmother whatever had passed at the ball , her two sisters knocked at the door , which Cinderella ran and opened .
19 `` How long you have stayed ! ''
20 cried she , gaping , rubbing her eyes and stretching herself as if she had been just waked out of her sleep ; she had not , however , any manner of inclination to sleep since they went from home .

2 The King 's son conducted her to the most honorable seat , and afterward took her out to dance with him ; she danced so very gracefully that they all more and more admired her .

5 While Cinderella was thus amusing her sisters , she heard the clock strike eleven and three-quarters , whereupon she immediately made a courtesy to the company and hasted away as fast as she could .
6 When she got home she ran to seek out her godmother , and , after having thanked her , she said she could not but heartily wish she might go next day to the ball , because the King 's son had desired her .
7 As she was eagerly telling her godmother whatever had passed at the ball , her two sisters knocked at the door , which Cinderella ran and opened .
8 `` How long you have stayed ! ''
9 cried she , gaping , rubbing her eyes and stretching herself as if she had been just waked out of her sleep ; she had not , however , any manner of inclination to sleep since they went from home .

11 There came thither the finest princess , the most beautiful ever was seen with mortal eyes ; she showed us a thousand civilities , and gave us oranges and citrons . ''
12 Cinderella seemed very indifferent in the matter ; indeed , she asked them the name of that princess ; but they told her they did not know it , and that the King 's son was very uneasy on her account and would give all the world to know who she was .

1 The King 's son conducted her to the most honorable seat , and afterward took her out to dance with him ; she danced so very gracefully that they all more and more admired her .
2 A fine collation was served up , whereof the young prince ate not a morsel , so intently was he busied in gazing on her .
3 She went and sat down by her sisters , showing them a thousand civilities , giving them part of the oranges and citrons which the Prince had presented her with , which very much surprised them , for they did not know her .
4 While Cinderella was thus amusing her sisters , she heard the clock strike eleven and three-quarters , whereupon she immediately made a courtesy to the company and hasted away as fast as she could .
5 When she got home she ran to seek out her godmother , and , after having thanked her , she said she could not but heartily wish she might go next day to the ball , because the King 's son had desired her .
6 As she was eagerly telling her godmother whatever had passed at the ball , her two sisters knocked at the door , which Cinderella ran and opened .
7 `` How long you have stayed ! ''
8 cried she , gaping , rubbing her eyes and stretching herself as if she had been just waked out of her sleep ; she had not , however , any manner of inclination to sleep since they went from home .
9 `` If thou hadst been at the ball , '' said one of her sisters , `` thou wouldst not have been tired with it .
10 There came thither the finest princess , the most beautiful ever was seen with mortal eyes ; she showed us a thousand civilities , and gave us oranges and citrons . ''
11 Cinderella seemed very indifferent in the matter ; indeed , she asked them the name of that princess ; but they told her they did not know it , and that the King 's son was very uneasy on her account and would give all the world to know who she was .
12 At this Cinderella , smiling , replied : `` She must , then , be very beautiful indeed ; how happy you have been !

6 Cinderella , indeed , expected well such answer , and was very glad of the refusal ; for she would have been sadly put to it if her sister had lent her what she asked for jestingly .
7 The next day the two sisters were at the ball , and so was Cinderella , but dressed more magnificently than before .
8 The King 's son was always by her , and never ceased his compliments and kind speeches to her ; to whom all this was so far from being tiresome that she quite forgot what her godmother had recommended to her ; so that she , at last , counted the clock striking twelve when she took it to be no more than eleven ; she then rose up and fled , as nimble as a deer .
9 The Prince followed , but could not overtake her .
10 She left behind one of her glass slippers , which the Prince took up most carefully .
11 She got home but quite out of breath , and in her nasty old clothes , having nothing left her of all her finery but one of the little slippers , fellow to that she dropped .
12 The guards at the palace gate were asked : If they had not seen a princess go out .
13 Who said : They had seen nobody go out but a young girl , very meanly dressed , and who had more the air of a poor country wench than a gentlewoman .
14 When the two sisters returned from the ball Cinderella asked them : If they had been well diverted , and if the fine lady had been there .
15 They told her : Yes , but that she hurried away immediately when it struck twelve , and with so much haste that she dropped one of her little glass slippers , the prettiest in the world , which the King 's son had taken up ; that he had done nothing but look at her all the time at the ball , and that most certainly he was very much in love with the beautiful person who owned the glass slipper .
16 What they said was very true ; for a few days after the King 's son caused it to be proclaimed , by sound of trumpet , that he would marry her whose foot the slipper would just fit .
17 They whom he employed began to try it upon the princesses , then the duchesses and all the Court , but in vain ; it was brought to the two sisters , who did all they possibly could to thrust their foot into the slipper , but they could not effect it .
18 Cinderella , who saw all this , and knew her slipper , said to them , laughing : `` Let me see if it will not fit me . ''
19 Her sisters burst out a-laughing , and began to banter her .
20 The gentleman who was sent to try the slipper looked earnestly at Cinderella , and , finding her very handsome , said : It was but just that she should try , and that he had orders to let everyone make trial .
21 He obliged Cinderella to sit down , and , putting the slipper to her XXXXX , he found it went on very easily , and fitted her as if it had been made of wax .	foot		Ah|Court|Miss|ball|breath|country|foot|lady|refusal|slipper

1 She left behind one of her glass slippers , which the Prince took up most carefully .
2 She got home but quite out of breath , and in her nasty old clothes , having nothing left her of all her finery but one of the little slippers , fellow to that she dropped .
3 The guards at the palace gate were asked : If they had not seen a princess go out .
4 Who said : They had seen nobody go out but a young girl , very meanly dressed , and who had more the air of a poor country wench than a gentlewoman .
5 When the two sisters returned from the ball Cinderella asked them : If they had been well diverted , and if the fine lady had been there .
6 They told her : Yes , but that she hurried away immediately when it struck twelve , and with so much haste that she dropped one of her little glass slippers , the prettiest in the world , which the King 's son had taken up ; that he had done nothing but look at her all the time at the ball , and that most certainly he was very much in love with the beautiful person who owned the glass slipper .
7 What they said was very true ; for a few days after the King 's son caused it to be proclaimed , by sound of trumpet , that he would marry her whose foot the slipper would just fit .
8 They whom he employed began to try it upon the princesses , then the duchesses and all the Court , but in vain ; it was brought to the two sisters , who did all they possibly could to thrust their foot into the slipper , but they could not effect it .
9 Cinderella , who saw all this , and knew her slipper , said to them , laughing : `` Let me see if it will not fit me . ''
10 Her sisters burst out a-laughing , and began to banter her .
11 The gentleman who was sent to try the slipper looked earnestly at Cinderella , and , finding her very handsome , said : It was but just that she should try , and that he had orders to let everyone make trial .
12 He obliged Cinderella to sit down , and , putting the slipper to her foot , he found it went on very easily , and fitted her as if it had been made of wax .
13 The astonishment her two sisters were in was excessively great , but still abundantly greater when Cinderella pulled out of her pocket the other slipper , and put it on her foot .
14 Thereupon , in came her godmother , who , having touched with her wand Cinderella 's clothes , made them richer and more magnificent than any of those she had before .
15 And now her two sisters found her to be that fine , beautiful lady whom they had seen at the ball .
16 They threw themselves at her feet to beg pardon for all the ill-treatment they had made her undergo .
17 Cinderella took them up , and , as she embraced them , cried : That she forgave them with all her heart , and desired them always to love her .
18 She was conducted to the young prince , dressed as she was ; he thought her more charming than ever , and , a few days after , married her .
19 Cinderella , who was no less good than beautiful , gave her two sisters lodgings in the palace , and that very same day matched them with two great lords of the Court .
20 -LRB- 1 -RRB- -LRB- 1 -RRB- Charles Perrault .

1 She left behind one of her glass slippers , which the Prince took up most carefully .
2 She got home but quite out of breath , and in her nasty old clothes , having nothing left her of all her finery but one of the little slippers , fellow to that she dropped .
3 The guards at the palace gate were asked : If they had not seen a princess go out .
4 Who said : They had seen nobody go out but a young girl , very meanly dressed , and who had more the air of a poor country wench than a gentlewoman .
5 When the two sisters returned from the ball Cinderella asked them : If they had been well diverted , and if the fine lady had been there .
6 They told her : Yes , but that she hurried away immediately when it struck twelve , and with so much haste that she dropped one of her little glass slippers , the prettiest in the world , which the King 's son had taken up ; that he had done nothing but look at her all the time at the ball , and that most certainly he was very much in love with the beautiful person who owned the glass slipper .
7 What they said was very true ; for a few days after the King 's son caused it to be proclaimed , by sound of trumpet , that he would marry her whose foot the slipper would just fit .
8 They whom he employed began to try it upon the princesses , then the duchesses and all the Court , but in vain ; it was brought to the two sisters , who did all they possibly could to thrust their foot into the slipper , but they could not effect it .
9 Cinderella , who saw all this , and knew her slipper , said to them , laughing : `` Let me see if it will not fit me . ''
10 Her sisters burst out a-laughing , and began to banter her .
11 The gentleman who was sent to try the slipper looked earnestly at Cinderella , and , finding her very handsome , said : It was but just that she should try , and that he had orders to let everyone make trial .
12 He obliged Cinderella to sit down , and , putting the slipper to her foot , he found it went on very easily , and fitted her as if it had been made of wax .
13 The astonishment her two sisters were in was excessively great , but still abundantly greater when Cinderella pulled out of her pocket the other slipper , and put it on her foot .
14 Thereupon , in came her godmother , who , having touched with her wand Cinderella 's clothes , made them richer and more magnificent than any of those she had before .
15 And now her two sisters found her to be that fine , beautiful lady whom they had seen at the ball .
16 They threw themselves at her feet to beg pardon for all the ill-treatment they had made her undergo .
17 Cinderella took them up , and , as she embraced them , cried : That she forgave them with all her heart , and desired them always to love her .
18 She was conducted to the young prince , dressed as she was ; he thought her more charming than ever , and , a few days after , married her .
19 Cinderella , who was no less good than beautiful , gave her two sisters lodgings in the palace , and that very same day matched them with two great lords of the Court .

17 They sat down by a fountain and the magician pulled a cake from his girdle , which he divided between them .
18 They then journeyed onward till they almost reached the mountains .
19 Aladdin was so tired that he begged to go back , but the magician beguiled him with pleasant stories , and led him on in spite of himself .
20 At last they came to two mountains divided by a narrow valley .
21 `` We will go no farther , '' said the false XXXXX .	uncle		LAMP|ball|bidding|boy|cake|country|day|likeness|uncle|wine

1 ALADDIN AND THE WONDERFUL LAMP There once lived a poor tailor , who had a son called Aladdin , a careless , idle boy who would do nothing but play ball all day long in the streets with little idle boys like himself .
2 This so grieved the father that he died ; yet , in spite of his mother 's tears and prayers , Aladdin did not mend his ways .
3 One day , when he was playing in the streets as usual , a stranger asked him his age , and if he was not the son of Mustapha the tailor .
4 `` I am , sir , '' replied Aladdin ; `` but he died a long while ago . ''
5 On this the stranger , who was a famous African magician , fell on his neck and kissed him , saying , `` I am your uncle , and knew you from your likeness to my brother .
6 Go to your mother and tell her I am coming . ''
7 Aladdin ran home and told his mother of his newly found uncle .
8 `` Indeed , child , '' she said , `` your father had a brother , but I always thought he was dead . ''
9 However , she prepared supper , and bade Aladdin seek his uncle , who came laden with wine and fruit .
10 He presently fell down and kissed the place where Mustapha used to sit , bidding Aladdin 's mother not to be surprised at not having seen him before , as he had been forty years out of the country .
11 He then turned to Aladdin , and asked him his trade , at which the boy hung his head , while his mother burst into tears .
12 On learning that Aladdin was idle and would learn no trade , he offered to take a shop for him and stock it with merchandise .
13 Next day he bought Aladdin a fine suit of clothes and took him all over the city , showing him the sights , and brought him home at nightfall to his mother , who was overjoyed to see her son so fine .
14 The next day the magician led Aladdin into some beautiful gardens a long way outside the city gates .
15 They sat down by a fountain and the magician pulled a cake from his girdle , which he divided between them .
16 They then journeyed onward till they almost reached the mountains .
17 Aladdin was so tired that he begged to go back , but the magician beguiled him with pleasant stories , and led him on in spite of himself .
18 At last they came to two mountains divided by a narrow valley .
19 `` We will go no farther , '' said the false uncle .
20 `` I will show you something wonderful ; only do you gather up sticks while I kindle a fire . ''
21 When it was lit the XXXXX threw on it a powder he had about him , at the same time saying some magical words .	magician		LAMP|boy|fountain|magician|mother|nothing|shop|something|stories|tears
